Catholicism</a></span></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Related ethnic groups</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<div title="Scottish people">Scottish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manx_people" title="Manx people">Manx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breton_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Breton people">Bretons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;padding-top:0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:left;">\n<hr /><sup><small>a</small></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cornish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish American">Cornish American</a>, <sup><small>b</small></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Australian" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish Australian">Cornish Australian</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Cornish people</b> or <b>Cornish</b> (<a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>: <i lang="kw">Kernowyon</i>) are a  <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic group</a> native to, or associated with <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a><sup id="cite_ref-Dic108_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dic108-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> and a recognised national minority in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> which can trace its roots to the <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">ancient Britons</a> who inhabited southern and central <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> before the <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">Roman conquest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Many in Cornwall today continue to assert a distinct identity separate from or in addition to <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a> or <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">British</a> identities. Cornish identity has been adopted by migrants into Cornwall, as well as by emigrant and descendant communities from Cornwall, the latter sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_diaspora" title="Cornish diaspora">Cornish diaspora</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jack1_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Although not included as an explicit option in the UK census, the numbers of those claiming Cornish ethnic and national identity are officially recognised and recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cornwall.gov.uk_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornwall.gov.uk-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Throughout <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>, the ancient Britons formed a series of <a href="/wiki/Tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Tribes">tribes</a>, cultures and identities in Great Britain; the <a href="/wiki/Dumnonii" title="Dumnonii">Dumnonii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornovii_(Cornish)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornovii (Cornish)">Cornovii</a> were the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Celtic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Celtic tribes">Celtic tribes</a> who inhabited what was to become Cornwall during the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sub-Roman_Britain" title="Sub-Roman Britain">post-Roman</a> periods.<sup id="cite_ref-Snyder160_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder160-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The name Cornwall and its <a href="/wiki/Demonym" title="Demonym">demonym</a> Cornish are derived from the Celtic Cornovii tribe.<sup id="cite_ref-Snyder160_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder160-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Magnag2_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnag2-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">Anglo-Saxon invasion and settlement of Britain</a> in the 5th to 6th centuries pushed Celtic culture and some Celtic peoples to the northern and western fringes of Britain. The Cornish people, who shared the <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic language</a> with the Welsh and Bretons across the sea, were referred to in the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> language as the "Westwalas" meaning West Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-Snyder160_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder160-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Deorham" title="Battle of Deorham">Battle of Deorham</a> between the Britons and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a> is thought to have resulted in a loss of landlinks with the people of Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Cornish people and their Brythonic <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish language</a> experienced a process of <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation" title="Anglicisation">anglicisation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">attrition</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Medieval</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Period" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern Period">early Modern Period</a>. By the 18th century, and following the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>, the Cornish language and identity had faded, largely replaced by the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> (albeit Cornish-influenced <a href="/wiki/West_Country_dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="West Country dialects">West Country dialects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Cornish" title="Anglo-Cornish">Anglo-Cornish</a>) and/or <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">British identity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rough360_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rough360-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Celtic_revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic revival">Celtic revival</a> during the early-20th century enabled a cultural self-consciousness in Cornwall that revitalised the Cornish language and roused the Cornish to express a distinctly Celtic heritage. The Cornish language was granted official recognition under the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a> in 2002,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and in 2014 the Cornish people were recognised and afforded protection by the UK Government under the <a href="/wiki/Framework_Convention_for_the_Protection_of_National_Minorities" title="Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities">Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 2011 census, the population of Cornwall, including the <a href="/wiki/Isles_of_Scilly" title="Isles of Scilly">Isles of Scilly</a>, was estimated to be 532,300.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cornish_self-government_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish self-government movement">Cornish self-government movement</a> has called for greater recognition of Cornish culture, politics and language, and urged that Cornish people be accorded greater status, exemplified by the call for them to be one of the listed ethnic groups in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">United Kingdom Census 2011</a> form.<sup id="cite_ref-revagain_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revagain-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Classification"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Classification</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#2011_UK_Census"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">2011 UK Census</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Schools_census_(PLASC)"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Schools census (PLASC)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Ancestral_roots"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Ancestral roots</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Industry,_revival_and_the_modern_period"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Industry, revival and the modern period</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Geographic_distribution"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Geographic distribution</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Australia"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Australia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Canada"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Canada</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Mexico"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Mexico</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#South_Africa"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">South Africa</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#United_States"><span class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">United States</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Language"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Language</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Literature_and_folklore"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Literature and folklore</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Performing_and_visual_arts"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Performing and visual arts</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Religion"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Cuisine"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Cuisine</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Sport"><span class="tocnumber">4.6</span> <span class="toctext">Sport</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Institutions_and_politics"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Institutions and politics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Footnotes"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Footnotes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">British nationality law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Ethnic groups in the United Kingdom">Ethnic groups in the United Kingdom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_ethnicity_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Classification of ethnicity in the United Kingdom">Classification of ethnicity in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_and_Cornish%3F.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The Union and Cornish flags fluttering in the wind, against a grey, cloudy sky." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/British_and_Cornish%3F.jpg/220px-British_and_Cornish%3F.jpg" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/British_and_Cornish%3F.jpg/330px-British_and_Cornish%3F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/British_and_Cornish%3F.jpg/440px-British_and_Cornish%3F.jpg 2x" data-file-width="442" data-file-height="454" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_and_Cornish%3F.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Flag of the United Kingdom">Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Piran%27s_Flag" title="Saint Piran&#39;s Flag">Cornish</a> flags.</div></div></div>\n<p>Both geographic and historical factors distinguish the Cornish as an ethnic group<sup id="cite_ref-Ther243_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ther243-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> further supported by identifiable genetic variance between the populations of Cornwall, neighbouring Devon and England as published in a 2012 Oxford University study.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Throughout medieval and <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern Britain">Early Modern Britain</a>, the Cornish were at some points accorded the same status as the English and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a> and considered a separate <a href="/wiki/Race_(classification_of_human_beings)" class="mw-redirect" title="Race (classification of human beings)">race</a> or nation, distinct from their neighbours, with their own language, society and customs.<sup id="cite_ref-Stolye1_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stolye1-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> A process of <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation" title="Anglicisation">Anglicisation</a> between 1485 and 1700 led to the Cornish adopting English language, culture and civic identity, a view reinforced by Cornish historian <a href="/wiki/A._L._Rowse" title="A. L. Rowse">A. L. Rowse</a> who said they were gradually "absorbed into the mainstream of English life".<sup id="cite_ref-Stolye1_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stolye1-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Although "decidedly modern" and "largely retrospective" in its <a href="/wiki/Identity_politics" title="Identity politics">identity politics</a>, Cornish and Celtic associations have advanced the notion of a distinct Cornish national and ethnic identity since the late 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi381_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi381-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">United Kingdom Census 2001</a>, despite no explicit "Cornish" option being available, approximately 34,000 people in Cornwall and 3,500 people elsewhere in the UK\xe2\x80\x94a combined total equal to nearly 7 per cent of the population of Cornwall\xe2\x80\x94identified themselves as ethnic Cornish by writing this in under the "other" ethnicity option.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Indy_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indy-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The census figures show a change in identity from West to East, in <a href="/wiki/Penwith" title="Penwith">Penwith</a> 9.2 per cent identified as ethnically Cornish, in <a href="/wiki/Kerrier" title="Kerrier">Kerrier</a> it was 7.5 per cent, in <a href="/wiki/Carrick,_Cornwall" title="Carrick, Cornwall">Carrick</a> 6.6 per cent, <a href="/wiki/Restormel" title="Restormel">Restormel</a> 6.3 per cent, <a href="/wiki/North_Cornwall" title="North Cornwall">North Cornwall</a> 6 per cent, and <a href="/wiki/Caradon" title="Caradon">Caradon</a> 5.6 per cent. Weighting of the 2001 Census data gives a figure of 154,791 people with Cornish ethnicity living in Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Cornish have been described as "a special case" in England, with an "ethnic rather than regional identity".<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Structural changes to the <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Politics of the United Kingdom">politics of the United Kingdom</a>, particularly the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a>, have been the cited as the main stimulus to "a growing interest in Cornish identity and distinctiveness" in late-20th century Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Stolye1_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stolye1-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The British are the citizens of the United Kingdom, a people who by convention consist of four national groups: the <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/People_of_Northern_Ireland" title="People of Northern Ireland">Northern Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stolye1_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stolye1-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In the 1990s it was said that the notion that the Cornish are to be classified as a nation comparable to the English, Irish, Scots and Welsh, "has practically vanished from the popular consciousness" outside Cornwall,<sup id="cite_ref-Stolye1_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stolye1-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and that, despite a "real and substantive" identity, the Cornish "struggle for recognition as a national group distinct from the English".<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi381_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi381-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>  However, in 2014, after a 15-year campaign, the UK government officially recognised the Cornish as a national minority under the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Framework_Convention_for_the_Protection_of_National_Minorities" title="Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities">Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities</a>, giving the Cornish the same status as the Welsh, Scots and Irish within the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Inhabitants of Cornwall may have multiple <a href="/wiki/Political" class="mw-redirect" title="Political">political</a> allegiances, adopting mixed, dual or hyphenated identities such as "Cornish first and British second",<sup id="cite_ref-revagain_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revagain-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> "Cornish and British and European",<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> or, like <a href="/wiki/Phil_Vickery_(rugby_union)" title="Phil Vickery (rugby union)">Phil Vickery</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> prop for the <a href="/wiki/England_national_rugby_union_team" title="England national rugby union team">England national rugby union team</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_and_Irish_Lions" title="British and Irish Lions">British and Irish Lions</a>), describe themselves as "Cornish" and "English".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, another international rugby union player, <a href="/wiki/Josh_Matavesi" title="Josh Matavesi">Josh Matavesi</a>, describes himself as Cornish-<a href="/wiki/Fijians" title="Fijians">Fijian</a> and Cornish not English.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A survey by <a href="/wiki/University_of_Plymouth" title="University of Plymouth">Plymouth University</a> in 2000 found that 30% of children in Cornwall felt "Cornish, not English".<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> A 2004 survey on national identity by the finance firm <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a> found that 44% of respondents in Cornwall saw themselves as Cornish rather than British or English.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> A 2008 <a href="/wiki/University_of_Exeter" title="University of Exeter">University of Exeter</a> study conducted in 16 towns across Cornwall found that 59% felt themselves to be Cornish and 41% felt "More Cornish than English", while for over a third of respondents the Cornish identity formed their primary <i>national</i> identity. Genealogy and family history were considered to be the chief criteria for \xe2\x80\x98being\xe2\x80\x99 Cornish, particularly among those who possessed such ties, while being born in Cornwall was also held to be important.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2008 study by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> of 15- and 16-year-old schoolchildren in Cornwall found that 58% of respondents felt themselves to be either \xe2\x80\x98Fairly\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98Very much\xe2\x80\x99 Cornish. The other 42% may be the result of in-migration to the area during the second half of the twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2010 study by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Exeter" title="University of Exeter">University of Exeter</a> into the meaning of contemporary Cornish identity across Cornwall found that there was a "west-east distance decay in the strength of the Cornish identity." The study was conducted amongst the farming community as they were deemed to be the socio-professional group most objectively representative of Cornishness. All participants categorised themselves as Cornish and identified Cornish as their primary ethnic group orientation. Those in the west primarily thought of themselves as Cornish and British/Celtic, while those in the east tended to think of themselves as Cornish and English. All participants in West Cornwall who identified as Cornish and not English described people in East Cornwall, without hesitation, as equally Cornish as themselves. Those who identified as Cornish and English stressed the primacy of their Cornishness and a capacity to distance themselves from their Englishness. Ancestry was seen as the most important criterion for being categorised as Cornish, above place of birth or growing up in Cornwall. This study supports a 1988 study by Mary McArthur that had found that the meanings of Cornishness varied substantially, from local to national identity. Both studies also observed that the Cornish were less materialistic than the English. The Cornish generally saw the English, or city people, as being "less friendly and more aggressively self-promoting and insensitive." The Cornish saw themselves as friendly, welcoming and caring.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2010 British Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, said "I think Cornish national identity is very powerful" and that his government would "devolve a lot of power to Cornwall \xe2\x80\x93 that will go to the <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">Cornish unitary authority</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2011_UK_Census">2011 UK Census</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 2011 UK Census">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Call_yourself_Cornish.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Call_yourself_Cornish.jpg/170px-Call_yourself_Cornish.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Call_yourself_Cornish.jpg/255px-Call_yourself_Cornish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Call_yourself_Cornish.jpg/340px-Call_yourself_Cornish.jpg 2x" data-file-width="517" data-file-height="694" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Call_yourself_Cornish.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A poster in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> telling people how to describe their ethnicity and national identity as Cornish in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 census</a></div></div></div>\n<p>A campaign for the inclusion of a Cornish tick-box in the nationality section of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 census</a> failed to win the support of parliament in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Indy_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indy-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> As a consequence, posters were created by the census organisation and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">Cornwall Council</a> which advised residents how they could identify themselves as Cornish by writing it in the national identity and ethnicity sections and record Cornish in the main language section.<sup id="cite_ref-cornwall.gov.uk_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornwall.gov.uk-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, people could record Cornwall as their country of birth.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Like other identities, Cornish has an allocated census code, (06), the same as for 2001,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> which applied and was counted throughout Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> People were first able to record their ethnicity as Cornish in the 2001 UK Census, and some 37,000 people did so by writing it in.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A total of 83,499 people in England and Wales were described as having a Cornish national identity. 59,456 of these were described as Cornish only, 6,261 as Cornish and British, and 17,782 as Cornish and at least one other identity, with or without British. Within Cornwall the total was 73,220 (14% of the population) with 52,793 (9.9%) as Cornish only, 5,185 (1%) as Cornish and British, and 15,242 (2.9%) as Cornish and at least one other identity, with or without British.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Scotland 467 people described themselves as having Cornish national identity. 254 with Cornish identity only, 39 as Scottish and Cornish, and 174 having Cornish identity and a least one other UK identity (excluding Scottish).<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Schools_census_.28PLASC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Schools_census_(PLASC)">Schools census (PLASC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Schools census (PLASC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since 2006 school children in Cornwall have been able to record themselves as ethnically Cornish on the annual Schools Census (PLASC). Since then the number identifying as Cornish has risen from 24% to 46%. The Department for Education recommends that parents and guardians determine the ethnicity of children at primary schools whilst pupils at secondary schools can decide their own ethnicity.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>2006: 23.7 percent \xe2\x80\x93 17,218 pupils out of 72,571</li>\n<li>2007: 27.3 percent \xe2\x80\x93 19,988 pupils out of 72,842<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2008: 30.3 percent \xe2\x80\x93 21,610 pupils out of 71,302</li>\n<li>2009: 33.9 percent \xe2\x80\x93 23,808 pupils out of 70,275</li>\n<li>2010: 37.2 percent \xe2\x80\x93 26,140 pupils out of 69,950<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2011: 40.9 percent \xe2\x80\x93 28,584 pupils out of 69,811<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>2012: 43.0 percent \xe2\x80\x93 30,181 pupils out of 69,909</li>\n<li>2013: 46.0 percent \xe2\x80\x93 32,254 pupils out of 70,097<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Cornwall" title="History of Cornwall">History of Cornwall</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancestral_roots">Ancestral roots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ancestral roots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_the_British_Isles" title="Genetic history of the British Isles">Genetic history of the British Isles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historical_immigration_to_Great_Britain" title="Historical immigration to Great Britain">Historical immigration to Great Britain</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tre_Pol_and_Pen" class="mw-redirect" title="Tre Pol and Pen">Tre Pol and Pen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornish_surnames" title="Cornish surnames">Cornish surnames</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Menantol1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="two weathered stones standing at an angle on a grassy hill, with a third doughnut-shaped stone between them" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Menantol1.jpg/220px-Menantol1.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Menantol1.jpg/330px-Menantol1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Menantol1.jpg/440px-Menantol1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Menantol1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/M%C3%AAn-an-Tol" title="M\xc3\xaan-an-Tol">M\xc3\xaan-an-Tol</a> is an ancient <a href="/wiki/Standing_stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Standing stone">lith</a> site in Cornwall</div></div></div>\n<p>Traditional accounts of Cornish ancestry teach that they are descended from the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a> making them distinct from the English, many (but not all) of whom are descended from the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a> who colonised Great Britain from their homelands in northern Europe and drove the Celts to Britain\'s western and northern fringes.<sup id="cite_ref-SnowCelt_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SnowCelt-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-United_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-United-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stolye2_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stolye2-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> However, some DNA research investigating the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_the_British_Isles" title="Genetic history of the British Isles">genetic history of the British Isles</a> suggests that three quarters of contemporary <a href="/wiki/Y-chromosomes" class="mw-redirect" title="Y-chromosomes">Y-chromosomes</a> of <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">British people</a>\xe2\x80\x94including the Cornish\xe2\x80\x94originate from <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> who settled in <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Europe" title="Atlantic Europe">Atlantic Europe</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> era,<sup id="cite_ref-United_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-United-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> "after the melting of the ice caps but before the land broke away from the mainland and divided into islands".<sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">Archaeological</a> evidence supports pre-historic human habitation in Cornwall, at least as early as the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Paleolithic" title="Lower Paleolithic">Lower Paleolithic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Overview_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overview-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Although there was a separation of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> from <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a> as a consequence of the last <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a>, genetic evidence indicates that the people of Great Britain broadly share a common ancestry with the <a href="/wiki/Basque_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Basque people">Basque people</a> who live in the <a href="/wiki/Basque_Country_(greater_region)" title="Basque Country (greater region)">Basque Country</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-United_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-United-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> On this basis, geneticist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Oppenheimer" title="Stephen Oppenheimer">Stephen Oppenheimer</a> suggests that the first settlers of Great Britain were unlikely to have spoken one of the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a>, but rather an ancient tongue related to the <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The next <a href="/wiki/Historical_immigration_to_Great_Britain" title="Historical immigration to Great Britain">historical immigration to Great Britain</a> occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period,<sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> interpreted by <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Sykes" title="Bryan Sykes">Bryan Sykes</a>\xe2\x80\x94professor of <a href="/wiki/Human_genetics" title="Human genetics">human genetics</a> at the University of Oxford\xe2\x80\x94as the arrival of the Celts from the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, and the origin of Britain\'s and Ireland\'s <a href="/wiki/List_of_Celtic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Celtic tribes">Celtic tribes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> It is these people who are considered the progenitors of the Cornish.<sup id="cite_ref-Magnag2_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnag2-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Overview_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overview-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> A study by the <a href="/wiki/Wellcome_Trust" title="Wellcome Trust">Wellcome Trust</a>, led by Sir <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bodmer" title="Walter Bodmer">Walter Bodmer</a> and published on Channel 4\'s <i>Faces of Britain</i> in April 2007, showed that the Cornish people have a particular variant of the <a href="/wiki/Melanocortin_1_receptor" title="Melanocortin 1 receptor">Melanocortin 1 receptor</a> gene, identifying them as Celts more closely related to the Welsh than to the English.<sup id="cite_ref-thegene_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thegene-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Within the areas of the UK studied, this gene appeared in 26 per cent of the population of <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>, 23 per cent of <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a>, 21 per cent of Wales, 16 per cent of Cornwall, 13 per cent of <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>, and 11 per cent of <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">North East England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thegene_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thegene-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> According to a DNA survey carried out for the Royal Society and published in 2012 by Peter Donnelly, professor of statistical science at Oxford University and director of the Wellcome Trust genetics centre, Cornish people, along with the Welsh and people of Devon,<sup id="cite_ref-theverge.com_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theverge.com-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> were found to be among the most genetically distinct groups in Britain. Others in England and Britain were found to have a greater range of genetic ancestries, with people from Cornwall being distinct from those elsewhere in England and the neighbouring population of Devon,<sup id="cite_ref-theverge.com_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theverge.com-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> though overall still very closely related to the \'English\' possibly due to their stronger genetic links to the tribes that arrived after the last ice age.<sup id="cite_ref-TeleCooke_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TeleCooke-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> However, a more recent study by Oxford University geneticist Professor Peter Donnelly suggests that the Cornish have DNA that is more similar to that of other English groups than to the Welsh or the Scots.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC18March2015_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC18March2015-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Both Cornish and Devonians have similar but distinct genetic profiles which show less continental \'German\' influence and more northern \'French\' material than the English and Scots; all showing a similar portion of "Nordic" material suggesting the idea of a uniform "Celtic" genetic race is false.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC18March2015_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC18March2015-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The British Isles appear on a pale and yellowed map. The isles are divided into political territories." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg/170px-Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg" width="170" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg/255px-Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg/340px-Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="685" data-file-height="985" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saxon_England_according_to_the_Saxon_Chronicle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 18th century map of Great Britain based on accounts from the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i>, showing "Cornweallas"</div></div></div>\n<p>Throughout <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> the Celts spoke <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a>, and formed a series of tribes, cultures and identities, notably the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaels</a> in the north and the <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Britons</a> in the south.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Britons were themselves a divided people;<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> although they shared the <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic languages</a>, they were tribal, and divided into regional societies, and within them sub-groups. Examples of these tribal societies were the <a href="/wiki/Brigantes" title="Brigantes">Brigantes</a> in the north, and the <a href="/wiki/Ordovices" title="Ordovices">Ordovices</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Demetae" title="Demetae">Demetae</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Silures" title="Silures">Silures</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Deceangli" title="Deceangli">Deceangli</a> in the west.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In the extreme southwest, what was to become Cornwall, were the <a href="/wiki/Dumnonii" title="Dumnonii">Dumnonii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornovii_(Cornwall)" title="Cornovii (Cornwall)">Cornovii</a>, who lived in the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Dumnonia" title="Dumnonia">Dumnonia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Snyder160_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder160-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">Roman conquest of Britain</a> in the 1st century introduced <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a> to Britain, who upon their arrival initially recorded the Dumnonii, but later reported on the Cornovii, who were possibly a sub-group of the Dumnonii.<sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Although the Romans colonised much of central and southern Britain, Dumnonia was "virtually unaffected" by the conquest;<sup id="cite_ref-Rough360_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rough360-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Overview_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overview-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Roman rule had little or no impact on the region,<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Magnag2_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnag2-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> meaning it could flourish as a semi- or fully independent kingdom which evidence shows was sometimes under the dominion of the <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Britons" title="King of the Britons">kings of the Britons</a>, and sometimes to have been governed by its own <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Dumnonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings of Dumnonia">Dumnonian monarchy</a>, either by the title of duke or king.<sup id="cite_ref-Brithist_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brithist-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Petty_kingdom" title="Petty kingdom">petty kingdom</a> shared strong linguistic, political and cultural links with <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, a peninsula on <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a> south of Cornwall inhabited by Britons; the Cornish and <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton languages</a> were nearly indistinguishable in this period, and both Cornwall and Brittany remain dotted with dedications to the same Celtic <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sny157_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sny157-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(410)" title="Sack of Rome (410)">Sack of Rome</a> in the year 410 prompted a complete <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">Roman departure from Britain</a>, and Cornwall then experienced an influx of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christian</a> missionaries from Ireland who had a profound effect upon the early Cornish people, their culture, faith and architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The ensuing <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">decline of the Roman Empire</a> encouraged the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_invasion_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon invasion of Britain">Anglo-Saxon invasion of Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Overview_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overview-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">Jutes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frisii" title="Frisii">Frisii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a> from northern Europe, established petty kingdoms and settled in different regions of what was to become England, and parts of southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, progressively defeating the Britons in battle. The Saxons of the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a> in particular were expanding their territory westwards towards Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The Cornish were frequently embattled with the West Saxons, who used their <a href="/wiki/Germanic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic language">Germanic</a> word <i><a href="/wiki/Walha" class="mw-redirect" title="Walha">walha</a></i> (modern English: Welsh) meaning "stranger" or "foreigner", to describe their opponents,<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth24-25_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth24-25-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> later specifying them as the <i>Westwalas</i> (West Welsh) or <i>Cornwalas</i> (the Cornish).<sup id="cite_ref-Snyder160_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder160-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Overview_62-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overview-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Conflict continued until King <a href="/wiki/Athelstan_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Athelstan of England">Athelstan of England</a> determined that the <a href="/wiki/River_Tamar" title="River Tamar">River Tamar</a> be the formal boundary between the West Saxons and the Cornish in the year 936,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> making Cornwall one of the last retreats of the Britons encouraging the development of a distinct Cornish identity;<sup id="cite_ref-Brithist_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brithist-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Brittonic culture in Britain became confined to Cornwall, parts of <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_West_England" title="North West England">North West England</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_West_Scotland" title="South West Scotland">South West Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SnowCelt_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SnowCelt-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stolye2_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stolye2-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Overview_62-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overview-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Although a treaty was agreed,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2012)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Anglo-Saxon political influence stretched westwards until some time in the late 10th century when "Cornwall was definitively incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglicisation_and_rebellion">Anglicisation and rebellion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Anglicisation and rebellion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Rebellion_of_1497" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish Rebellion of 1497">Cornish Rebellion of 1497</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prayer_Book_Rebellion" title="Prayer Book Rebellion">Prayer Book Rebellion</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_map_998.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Europe_map_998.PNG/220px-Europe_map_998.PNG" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Europe_map_998.PNG/330px-Europe_map_998.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Europe_map_998.PNG/440px-Europe_map_998.PNG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="554" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_map_998.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>European nations in <a href="/wiki/AD" class="mw-redirect" title="AD">AD</a> 998</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a>, which began with an invasion by the troops of William, Duke of Normandy (later, King <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of England">William I of England</a>) in 1066, resulted in the removal of the Anglo-Saxon derived monarchy, aristocracy, and clerical hierarchy and its replacement by <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a>, Scandinavian Vikings from northern France<sup id="cite_ref-Thom3_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thom3-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-state_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and their Breton allies, who, in many cases, maintained rule in the Brittonic-speaking parts of the conquered lands.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Shire" title="Shire">shires</a> of England were progressively divided amongst the <a href="/wiki/Companions_of_William_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Companions of William I of England">companions of William I of England</a>, who served as England\'s new nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-Thom3_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thom3-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The English would come to absorb the Normans,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> but the Cornish "vigorously resisted" their influence.<sup id="cite_ref-Mina482_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina482-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> At the time of the conquest, Cornwall was under the governance of <a href="/wiki/Cadoc_of_Cornwall" title="Cadoc of Cornwall">Cadoc of Cornwall</a>, the last Earl of Cornwall to be directly descended from the ancient monarchy of Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Earldom_of_Cornwall" class="mw-redirect" title="Earldom of Cornwall">Earldom of Cornwall</a> had held <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved</a> semi-<a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a> from England,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> but in 1067 was granted to <a href="/wiki/Robert,_Count_of_Mortain" title="Robert, Count of Mortain">Robert, Count of Mortain</a>, King William I\'s half-brother, and ruled thereafter by an <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">Anglo-Norman</a> aristocracy;<sup id="cite_ref-Magnag2_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnag2-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a></i>, the record of the great survey of England completed in 1086, "virtually all" landowners in Cornwall "had English names, making it impossible to be sure who was Cornish and who was English by race".<sup id="cite_ref-Orme6_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orme6-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> However, there was a persistent and "continuing differentiation" between the English and Cornish peoples during the Middle Ages, as evidenced by documents such as the 1173 <a href="/wiki/Charter" title="Charter">charter</a> of <a href="/wiki/Truro" title="Truro">Truro</a> which made explicit mention of both peoples as distinct.<sup id="cite_ref-Corn1066_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corn1066-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Earldom of Cornwall passed to various English <a href="/wiki/Nobles" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobles">nobles</a> throughout the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> but in 1337 the earldom was given the status of a <a href="/wiki/Duchy" title="Duchy">duchy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Edward,_the_Black_Prince" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward, the Black Prince">Edward, the Black Prince</a>, the first son and heir of King <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III of England</a>, became the first <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Cornwall" title="Duke of Cornwall">Duke of Cornwall</a> as a means for the prince to raise his own capital.<sup id="cite_ref-Corn1066_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corn1066-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Large parts of Cornwall were owned by Edward, 1st Duke of Cornwall, and successive English Dukes of Cornwall became the largest landowners in Cornwall;<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Monarchy of England">monarchy of England</a> established two special administrative institutions in Cornwall, the first being the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Cornwall" title="Duchy of Cornwall">Duchy of Cornwall</a> (one of only two in the Kingdom of England)<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and the second being the Cornish <a href="/wiki/Stannary_Courts_and_Parliaments" class="mw-redirect" title="Stannary Courts and Parliaments">Stannary Courts and Parliaments</a> (which governed Cornwall\'s tin industry).<sup id="cite_ref-Brithist_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brithist-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> These two institutions allowed "ordinary Cornish people to believe that they had been granted a unique constitutional status to reflect their unique cultural identity".<sup id="cite_ref-Stolye2_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stolye2-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> However, the Duchy of Cornwall gradually lost its political autonomy from England, a state which became increasingly centralised in London,<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and by the early-<a href="/wiki/Tudor_period" title="Tudor period">Tudor period</a> the Cornish had begun to see themselves as "a conquered people whose culture, liberties, and prosperity had been downgraded by the English".<sup id="cite_ref-Coop54_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coop54-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> This view was exacerbated in the 1490s by heavy taxation imposed by King <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII of England</a> upon the impoverished Cornish to raise funds for his military campaigns against King <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV of Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perkin_Warbeck" title="Perkin Warbeck">Perkin Warbeck</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Coop54_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coop54-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> as well as Henry VII\'s suspension of the privileges of the Cornish Stannaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Flet21_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flet21-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Having provided "more than their fair share of soldiers and sailors" for the conflict in <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">northern England</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Flet21_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flet21-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> and feeling aggrieved at "Cornwall\'s status as England\'s poorest county",<sup id="cite_ref-Coop54_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coop54-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Popular_revolt_in_late_medieval_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Popular revolt in late medieval Europe">popular uprising</a> out of Cornwall ensued\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Rebellion_of_1497" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish Rebellion of 1497">Cornish Rebellion of 1497</a>. The rebellion was initially a political march from <a href="/wiki/St_Keverne" title="St Keverne">St Keverne</a> to London led by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Flamank" title="Thomas Flamank">Thomas Flamank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_An_Gof" title="Michael An Gof">Michael An Gof</a>, motivated by a "mixture of reasons"; to raise money for charity; to celebrate their community; to present their grievances to the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Coop54_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coop54-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flet21_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flet21-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> but gathered pace across the <a href="/wiki/West_Country" title="West Country">West Country</a> as a revolt against the king.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornish_Language_Shift.svg" class="image"><img alt="A colour-coded map of Cornwall, surrounded by a blue sea. Cornwall is shaded dark red in the east and pale pink in the west, with a range of intermediate shades of red between, intended to represent periods of time in which the Cornish language was used." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Cornish_Language_Shift.svg/300px-Cornish_Language_Shift.svg.png" width="300" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Cornish_Language_Shift.svg/450px-Cornish_Language_Shift.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Cornish_Language_Shift.svg/600px-Cornish_Language_Shift.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1263" data-file-height="1003" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornish_Language_Shift.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish language</a> experienced a <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">shift</a> between 1300 and 1750, with the Cornish people gradually adopting English as their common language.</div></div></div>\n<p>Cornish was the most widely spoken language west of the River Tamar until around the mid-1300s, when <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> began to be adopted as a common language of the Cornish people.<sup id="cite_ref-Overview_62-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overview-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> As late as 1542 <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Boorde" title="Andrew Boorde">Andrew Boorde</a>, an English traveller, physician and writer, wrote that in Cornwall there were two languages, "Cornysshe" and "Englysshe", but that "there may be many men and women" in Cornwall who could not understand English.<sup id="cite_ref-Corn1066_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corn1066-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> While the <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">Norman language</a> was in use by much of the English aristocracy, Cornish was used as a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i>, particularly in the remote far west of Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Many Cornish <a href="/wiki/Landed_gentry" title="Landed gentry">landed gentry</a> chose mottos in the Cornish language for their <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coat of arms</a>, highlighting its socially high status.<sup id="cite_ref-Tan226_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tan226-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> However, in 1549 and following the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a>, King <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI of England</a> commanded that the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> liturgical text in the English language, should be introduced to all churches in his kingdom, meaning that <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and Celtic customs and services should be discontinued.<sup id="cite_ref-Overview_62-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overview-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Prayer_Book_Rebellion" title="Prayer Book Rebellion">Prayer Book Rebellion</a> was a militant revolt in Cornwall and parts of neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Uniformity_1549" title="Act of Uniformity 1549">Act of Uniformity 1549</a>, which outlawed all languages from church services apart from English,<sup id="cite_ref-Pitt122_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pitt122-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> and is specified as a testament to the affection and loyalty the Cornish people held for the Cornish language.<sup id="cite_ref-Tan226_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tan226-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In the rebellion, separate risings occurred simultaneously in <a href="/wiki/Bodmin" title="Bodmin">Bodmin</a> in Corwall, and <a href="/wiki/Sampford_Courtenay" title="Sampford Courtenay">Sampford Courtenay</a> in Devon\xe2\x80\x94which would both converge at <a href="/wiki/Exeter" title="Exeter">Exeter</a>, laying siege to the region\'s largest Protestant city.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> However, the rebellion was suppressed, thanks largely to the aid of foreign mercenaries in a series of battles in which "hundreds were killed",<sup id="cite_ref-Magnag2_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnag2-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> effectively ending Cornish as the common language of the Cornish people.<sup id="cite_ref-Overview_62-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overview-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Corn1066_90-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corn1066-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The Anglicanism of the Reformation served as a vehicle for Anglicisation in Cornwall; Protestantism had a lasting cultural effect upon the Cornish by way of linking Cornwall more closely with England, while lessening political and linguistic ties with the <a href="/wiki/Breton_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Breton people">Bretons</a> of Brittany.<sup id="cite_ref-Tann230_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tann230-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>, a series of armed conflicts and political machinations between <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Parliamentarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cavalier" title="Cavalier">Royalists</a>, polarised the populations of England and Wales. However, <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_in_the_English_Civil_War" title="Cornwall in the English Civil War">Cornwall in the English Civil War</a> was a staunchly Royalist enclave, an "important focus of support for the Royalist cause".<sup id="cite_ref-Price113_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price113-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Cornish soldiers were used as scouts and spies during the war, for their language was not understood by English Parliamentarians.<sup id="cite_ref-Price113_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price113-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The peace that followed the close of the war led to a further shift to the English language by the Cornish people, which encouraged an influx of English people to Cornwall. By the mid-17th century the use of the Cornish language had retreated far enough west to prompt concern and investigation by <a href="/wiki/Antiquarian" title="Antiquarian">antiquarians</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/William_Scawen" title="William Scawen">William Scawen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tann230_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tann230-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Price113_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price113-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> As the Cornish language diminished the people of Cornwall underwent a process of English <a href="/wiki/Enculturation" title="Enculturation">enculturation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Hect64_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hect64-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> becoming "absorbed into the mainstream of English life".<sup id="cite_ref-Stolye1_36-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stolye1-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Industry.2C_revival_and_the_modern_period"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry,_revival_and_the_modern_period">Industry, revival and the modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Industry, revival and the modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Celtic_revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic revival">Celtic revival</a> and <a href="/wiki/Home_Nations" title="Home Nations">Home Nations</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornish_National_Tartan.png" class="image"><img alt="A square consisting of crossed lines of vivid colours. Yellow and black form thick, crossed lines producing large squares of colour, intersected by thinner lines of white, blue and red. The design is symmetrical and repeating." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Cornish_National_Tartan.png/220px-Cornish_National_Tartan.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Cornish_National_Tartan.png/330px-Cornish_National_Tartan.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Cornish_National_Tartan.png/440px-Cornish_National_Tartan.png 2x" data-file-width="560" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornish_National_Tartan.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The National Tartan of Cornwall. <a href="/wiki/Cornish_kilts_and_tartans" title="Cornish kilts and tartans">Cornish kilts and tartans</a> are emblematic of a resurgent, <a href="/wiki/Pan-Celticism" title="Pan-Celticism">pan-Celtic</a> Cornish identity developed during Cornwall\'s <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Revival" title="Celtic Revival">Celtic Revival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> had a major impact upon the Cornish people.<sup id="cite_ref-Hect64_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hect64-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacLeod343_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod343-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Cornwall\'s economy was fully integrated into England\'s,<sup id="cite_ref-Hect64_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hect64-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Cornwall" class="mw-redirect" title="Mining in Cornwall">mining in Cornwall</a>, always an important source of employment and stability of the Cornish, experienced a process of industrialisation resulting in 30 per cent of Cornwall\'s adult population being employed by its mines.<sup id="cite_ref-MacLeod343_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod343-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> During this period, efforts were made by Cornish engineers to design <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engines</a> with which to power water pumps for Cornish mines thus aiding the extraction of mineral ore.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Industrial scale tin and copper mining operations in Cornwall melded Cornish identity with engines and heavy industry,<sup id="cite_ref-MacLeod343_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod343-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> and Cornwall\'s leading <a href="/wiki/Mining_engineer" class="mw-redirect" title="Mining engineer">mining engineer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Trevithick" title="Richard Trevithick">Richard Trevithick</a>, became "as much a part of Cornwall\'s heritage as any legendary giant from its Celtic past".<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Trevithick\'s most significant success was a high-pressure steam engine used to pump water and refuse from mines, but he was also the builder of the first full-scale working railway <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MuseumWales_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MuseumWales-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> On 21 February 1804, the world\'s first locomotive-hauled railway journey took place as Trevithick\'s unnamed steam locomotive hauled a train along the <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport" title="Rail transport">tramway</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Penydarren" title="Penydarren">Penydarren</a> ironworks, near <a href="/wiki/Merthyr_Tydfil" title="Merthyr Tydfil">Merthyr Tydfil</a> in Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-MuseumWales_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MuseumWales-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The construction of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Western_Railway" title="Great Western Railway">Great Western Railway</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> allowed for an influx of tourists to Cornwall from across Great Britain. Well into the <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian era</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interwar_period" title="Interwar period">interwar period</a>, Cornwall was branded as a rural retreat, a "primitive land of magic and romance", and as an "earlier incarnation of Englishness, a place more English than an England ravaged by modernity".<sup id="cite_ref-Cubitt164_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cubitt164-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Cornwall, the United Kingdom\'s only region with a <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropical-like climate</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Minahan180_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minahan180-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> became a centre for <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_England" title="Tourism in England">English tourism</a>, its coastline dominated by <a href="/wiki/Resort_town" title="Resort town">resort towns</a> increasingly composed of <a href="/wiki/Bungalow" title="Bungalow">bungalows</a> and <a href="/wiki/Villa" title="Villa">villas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cubitt164_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cubitt164-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Tourists visiting Cornwall are called <i>emmets</i> by locals, a Cornish language word for insects, referring to the creatures that the visitors resemble when swarming Cornwall\'s resorts.<sup id="cite_ref-Minahan180_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minahan180-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Nichols_Thom" title="John Nichols Thom">John Nichols Thom</a>, or Mad Tom, (1799 \xe2\x80\x93 31 May 1838) was a Cornishman self-declared messiah who, in the 19th century led the last battle to be fought on English soil, known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bossenden_Wood" title="Battle of Bossenden Wood">Battle of Bossenden Wood</a>. While not akin to the Cornish rebellions of the past, he did attract some Cornish support as well as mostly Kentish labourers, although his support was primarily of religious followers.\n</p><p>In the latter half of the 19th century Cornwall experienced rapid deindustrialisation,<sup id="cite_ref-Far_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Far-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> with the closure of mines in particular considered by the Cornish to be both an economic and cultural disaster.<sup id="cite_ref-Minahan180_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minahan180-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> This, coupled with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Romantic_nationalism" title="Romantic nationalism">Romantic nationalism</a> in Europe inspired and influenced a <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Revival" title="Celtic Revival">Celtic Revival</a> in Cornwall,<sup id="cite_ref-Far_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Far-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> a social, linguistic and artistic movement interested in Cornish medieval <a href="/wiki/Ethnology" title="Ethnology">ethnology</a>. This Revivalist upsurge investigated Cornwall\'s pre-industrial culture, using the Cornish language as the "principal badge of [Cornish] nationality and ethnic kinship\xe2\x80\x9d.<sup id="cite_ref-Far_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Far-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The first effective revival of Cornish began in 1904 when <a href="/wiki/Henry_Jenner" title="Henry Jenner">Henry Jenner</a>, a Celtic language enthusiast, published his book <i>Handbook of the Cornish Language</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball644_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball644-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unified_Cornish" title="Unified Cornish">Unified Cornish</a>, was based on Cornish as it was spoken in the 18th century, although his pupil <a href="/wiki/Robert_Morton_Nance" title="Robert Morton Nance">Robert Morton Nance</a> later steered the revival more towards the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Cornish" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Cornish">Middle Cornish</a> that had been used in the 16th century, before the language became influenced by English.<sup id="cite_ref-mina486_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mina486-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Visit_of_King_George_IV_to_Scotland" title="Visit of King George IV to Scotland">visit of King George IV to Scotland</a> in 1822 reinvigorated <a href="/wiki/Scottish_national_identity" title="Scottish national identity">Scottish national identity</a>, melding it with
