romanticist notions of <a href="/wiki/Tartan" title="Tartan">tartan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kilt" title="Kilt">kilts</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Scottish Highlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pitt181_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pitt181-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Pan-Celticism" title="Pan-Celticism">Pan-Celticism</a> gathered pace in the early 20th century, Cornishman <a href="/wiki/L._C._R._Duncombe-Jewell" title="L. C. R. Duncombe-Jewell">L. C. R. Duncombe-Jewell</a> and the Cowethas Kelto-Kernuak (a Cornish language interest group) asserted the use of <a href="/wiki/Cornish_kilts_and_tartans" title="Cornish kilts and tartans">Cornish kilts and tartans</a> as a "national dress ... common to all Celtic countries".<sup id="cite_ref-pitt181_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pitt181-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In 1924 the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Old_Cornwall_Societies" title="Federation of Old Cornwall Societies">Federation of Old Cornwall Societies</a> was formed to facilitate, preserve and maintain Celticity in Cornwall,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> followed by the similar <a href="/wiki/Gorseth_Kernow" class="mw-redirect" title="Gorseth Kernow">Gorseth Kernow</a> in 1928,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> and the formation of the Cornish nationalist political party <a href="/wiki/Mebyon_Kernow" title="Mebyon Kernow">Mebyon Kernow</a> in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-Mebyon_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mebyon-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Increased interest and communication across the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_nations" title="Celtic nations">Celtic nations</a> in Celtic languages and culture during the 1960s and 1970s spurred on the popularisation of the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_self-government_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish self-government movement">Cornish self-government movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Minahan180_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minahan180-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Since <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a> in Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland, enthusiasts for Cornish culture have pressed for the Cornish language to be taught formally in Cornish schools, while Cornish nationalists have demanded greater political autonomy for Cornwall, for example that it be constituted as the <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">United Kingdom\'s fifth consistuent country</a> with its own <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Assembly" title="Cornish Assembly">Cornish Assembly</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Minahan180_110-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minahan180-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_diaspora" title="Cornish diaspora">Cornish diaspora</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornish_miners_-_1866.png" class="image"><img alt="Two men wearing mining attire look at one-another in this black line drawing. Both wear dark clothing and mining helmets. The man on the right holds a long tool." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Cornish_miners_-_1866.png/170px-Cornish_miners_-_1866.png" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Cornish_miners_-_1866.png/255px-Cornish_miners_-_1866.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Cornish_miners_-_1866.png/340px-Cornish_miners_-_1866.png 2x" data-file-width="353" data-file-height="465" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornish_miners_-_1866.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cornish miners in the mid-19th century. A demise in <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Cornwall" class="mw-redirect" title="Mining in Cornwall">mining in Cornwall</a> prompted an exodus of Cornish miners and families resulting in a displaced <a href="/wiki/Cornish_diaspora" title="Cornish diaspora">Cornish diaspora</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Cornish people are concentrated in Cornwall, but after the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a> in the early modern period  were involved in the <a href="/wiki/British_colonisation_of_the_Americas" class="mw-redirect" title="British colonisation of the Americas">British colonisation of the Americas</a> and other transcontinental and <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_migrations" title="Transatlantic migrations">transatlantic migrations</a>. Initially, the number of migrants was comparatively small, with those who left Cornwall typically settling in North America or else amongst the ports and <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantations</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jack1_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the first half of the 19th century, the Cornish people were leaders in <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> and copper smelting, while <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Cornwall" class="mw-redirect" title="Mining in Cornwall">mining in Cornwall</a> was the people\'s major occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Increased competition from Australia, <a href="/wiki/British_Malaya" title="British Malaya">British Malaya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, coupled with the depletion of mineral deposits  brought about an economic decline for Cornish mining lasting half a century, and prompting mass <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">human migration</a> from Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-Jack1_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In each decade from 1861 to 1901, "around 20% of the Cornish male population migrated abroad"\xe2\x80\x94three times that of the average of <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>\xe2\x80\x94and totalling over a quarter of a million people lost to emigration between 1841 and 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-Jack1_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> There was a displacement of skilled Cornish engineers, farmers, merchants, miners and tradesmen, but their commercial and occupational expertise, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Underground_mining_(hard_rock)" title="Underground mining (hard rock)">hard rock mining</a>, was highly valued by the communities they met.<sup id="cite_ref-Jack1_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Within Great Britain, Cornish families were attracted from Cornwall to <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">North East England</a>\xe2\x80\x94particularly on <a href="/wiki/Teesside" title="Teesside">Teesside</a>\xe2\x80\x94to partake in coal mining as a means to earn wealth by using their mining skill. This has resulted in a concentration of Cornish names on and around Teesside that persists into the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-Tee_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tee-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Large numbers of the 19th century Cornish emigrants eventually returned to Cornwall, whilst the rate of emigration from Cornwall declined after World War I.<sup id="cite_ref-Willi143_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willi143-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> However, the global connections of the remaining <a href="/wiki/Cornish_diaspora" title="Cornish diaspora">Cornish diaspora</a>, which is concentrated in <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="English-speaking countries">English-speaking countries</a> such as Australia, Canada, South Africa and the United States, are "very strong".<sup id="cite_ref-Jack2_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack2-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jack1_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mina482_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina482-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jack3_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack3-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Their outreach has contributed to the international spread of <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a>, a movement within <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> Christianity that was popular with the Cornish people at the time of their mass migration.<sup id="cite_ref-Hemp27_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hemp27-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> "Cousin Jacks" is a nickname for the overseas Cornish, thought to derive from the practice of Cornishmen asking if job vacancies could be filled by their cousin named Jack in Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-Ther243_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ther243-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jupp229_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jupp229-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Australian" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish Australian">Cornish Australian</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harold_Oliver_zoom.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Harold_Oliver_zoom.png/200px-Harold_Oliver_zoom.png" width="200" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Harold_Oliver_zoom.png/300px-Harold_Oliver_zoom.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1a/Harold_Oliver_zoom.png 2x" data-file-width="334" data-file-height="381" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harold_Oliver_zoom.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Harold_Oliver_(Australian_footballer)" title="Harold Oliver (Australian footballer)">William "Harold" Oliver</a> was the son of Australian Cornish immigrants who lived in the mining town of <a href="/wiki/Waukaringa,_South_Australia" title="Waukaringa, South Australia">Waukaringa</a>. Harold Oliver was a three time national champion with the <a href="/wiki/Port_Adelaide_Football_Club" title="Port Adelaide Football Club">Port Adelaide Football Club</a> in 1910, 1913 and 1914.</div></div></div>\n<p>From the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Australia_(1788%E2%80%931850)" title="History of Australia (1788\xe2\x80\x931850)">beginning of Australia\'s colonial period</a> until after the Second World War, people from the United Kingdom made up a large majority of people coming to Australia, meaning that many Australian-born people can trace their origins to Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-ausbrit_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ausbrit-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The Cornish people in particular were actively encouraged to emigrate to Australia following the demise of <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Cornwall" class="mw-redirect" title="Mining in Cornwall">Cornish mining</a> in the 19th century. A "vigorous recruiting campaign" was launched to encourage the Cornish to aid with <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Australia" title="Mining in Australia">mining in Australia</a> because of their experience and expertise.<sup id="cite_ref-Jupp228_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jupp228-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Free passage to South Australia in particular was granted to hundreds of Cornish miners and their families,<sup id="cite_ref-Jupp228_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jupp228-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> so much so, that a large Cornish community gathered in Australia\'s <a href="/wiki/Copper_Coast" title="Copper Coast">Copper Coast</a>, and South Australia\'s <a href="/wiki/Yorke_Peninsula" title="Yorke Peninsula">Yorke Peninsula</a> became known as "Little Cornwall\xe2\x80\x9d.<sup id="cite_ref-Jack2_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack2-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It has been estimated between 1837 and 1840, 15 per cent of all assisted migrants to South Australia were Cornish.<sup id="cite_ref-Jupp228_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jupp228-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cornish settlement impacted upon social, cultural and religious life throughout the <a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Australia" title="History of South Australia">history of South Australia</a>. Cornish identity was embraced strongly in the Yorke Peninsula, but also in the more outlying mining towns of <a href="/wiki/Kapunda,_South_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Kapunda, South Australia">Kapunda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burra,_South_Australia" title="Burra, South Australia">Burra</a>, where Cornish miners constituted a sizeable community.<sup id="cite_ref-Jupp231_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jupp231-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a>, was the main form of religious practice for the Cornish. Methodist sensibilities were held with strong conviction by the migrant Cornish in a direct rivalry with Catholic <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish people</a> in Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-Jupp231_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jupp231-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kernewek_Lowender" title="Kernewek Lowender">Kernewek Lowender</a> is the largest Cornish festival in the world, held in the <a href="/wiki/Kadina,_South_Australia" title="Kadina, South Australia">Kadina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moonta,_South_Australia" title="Moonta, South Australia">Moonta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wallaroo,_South_Australia" title="Wallaroo, South Australia">Wallaroo</a> towns on the Yorke Peninsula, which attracts tens of thousands of visitors bi-annually.<sup id="cite_ref-Jack3_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack3-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jupp229_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jupp229-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Canadian" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish Canadian">Cornish Canadian</a></div>\n<p>European fishing ventures in and around <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a> during the 16th century were the earliest Cornish activity in what was to become Canada. However, permanent settlement by the Cornish across the Atlantic Ocean was rare until at least the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/British_colonisation_of_the_Americas" class="mw-redirect" title="British colonisation of the Americas">British colonisation of the Americas</a> encouraged additional migration of the Cornish to <a href="/wiki/The_Canadas" title="The Canadas">the Canadas</a>, particularly by those who served in Great Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The creation of the colony of <a href="/wiki/British_North_America" title="British North America">British North America</a> spurred more people from Cornwall to settle in North America; they were registered as English migrants.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Many Cornish (and other West Country) immigrants who had been agricultural labourers settled in an area of what is now South Central Ontario in what were the counties of Northumberland, Durham and Ontario, ranging from the towns of Port Hope and Cobourg in the east, to Whitby in the west and to the north ends of those counties.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/White_Mexican" class="mw-redirect" title="White Mexican">White Mexican</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TowerAcostaMine.JPG" class="image"><img alt="A dark angular structure viewed from its base upwards fills the scene. The sky appears light-grey and cloudy. The structure is made of a dark metal frame surmounted by platform." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/TowerAcostaMine.JPG/170px-TowerAcostaMine.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/TowerAcostaMine.JPG/255px-TowerAcostaMine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/TowerAcostaMine.JPG/340px-TowerAcostaMine.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TowerAcostaMine.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Silver_mining" title="Silver mining">silver mining</a> museum in <a href="/wiki/Mineral_del_Monte" title="Mineral del Monte">Mineral del Monte</a>, a remnant of the Cornish migration to <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> during the early-19th century.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1825 a band of 60 Cornishmen left <a href="/wiki/Falmouth,_Cornwall" title="Falmouth, Cornwall">Falmouth</a> for <a href="/wiki/Mineral_del_Monte" title="Mineral del Monte">Mineral del Monte</a> in central <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> with 1,500 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> (1,500 <a href="/wiki/Long_ton" title="Long ton">long tons</a>; 1,700 <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a>) of mining machinery with which to apply their mining skill and technologies to resuscitate Mexico\'s ailing <a href="/wiki/Silver_mining" title="Silver mining">silver mining</a> industry after the neglect caused by the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_War_of_Independence" title="Mexican War of Independence">Mexican War of Independence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CornMex_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CornMex-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Following their sea voyage they attempted to dock at <a href="/wiki/Veracruz,_Veracruz" class="mw-redirect" title="Veracruz, Veracruz">Veracruz</a> but were forced away by the Spanish to a beach at Mocambo from where they hauled their machinery through jungle and swamp to <a href="/w/index.php?title=Santa_Fe,_Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Santa Fe, Veracruz (page does not exist)">Santa Fe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CornMex_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CornMex-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> During this haul through the jungle, the Cornishmen and their Mexican helpers fell victim to <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>, resulting in 30 Cornish and 100 Mexican fatalities.<sup id="cite_ref-CornMex_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CornMex-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The fever forced the survivors to abandon their equipment and head inland up into the mountains to Xalapa to try and escape the mosquitos for three months, until the end of the rainy season. Once the rainy season closed the Cornish and Mexican miners continued their 250-mile (402&#160;km) "Great Trek" to Mineral del Monte, transporting their machinery to an altitude of 10,000 feet (3,048&#160;m) above sea level and arriving at their destination on 1 May 1826.<sup id="cite_ref-CornMex_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CornMex-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Following their arrival, the Cornish community flourished and stayed in central Mexico until the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">Mexican Revolution</a> in 1910. Although the Cornish community in Mexico broadly returned to Cornwall, they left a cultural legacy; <a href="/wiki/International_Pasty_Festival" title="International Pasty Festival">Cornish pasties</a>, Cornish mining museums and a <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Mexican_Cultural_Society" title="Cornish Mexican Cultural Society">Cornish Mexican Cultural Society</a> are all part of the local heritage and tradition in and around Mineral del Monte.<sup id="cite_ref-CornMex_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CornMex-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">South Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: South Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/White_South_African" class="mw-redirect" title="White South African">White South African</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Witwatersrand_Gold_Rush" title="Witwatersrand Gold Rush">Witwatersrand Gold Rush</a> of 1886 encouraged large numbers of Cornish miners to migrate to the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">South African Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Willi142_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willi142-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Although an international gold rush, the Cornish overwhelmingly formed the skilled labour force in the <a href="/wiki/Witwatersrand" title="Witwatersrand">Witwatersrand</a>, until the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a> prompted a retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-Willi142_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willi142-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish American">Cornish American</a></div>\n<p>The discovery of <a href="/wiki/Lead_ore" class="mw-redirect" title="Lead ore">lead ore</a> and copper in North America prompted an influx of Cornish miners to the continent, particularly around the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Mississippi_River" title="Upper Mississippi River">Upper Mississippi River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> By the early 19th century Cornish people were present in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Upper Peninsula of Michigan">Upper Peninsula of Michigan</a>\xe2\x80\x94particularly the mining town of <a href="/wiki/Ishpeming,_Michigan" title="Ishpeming, Michigan">Ishpeming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Additional waves of Cornish migrants followed the <a href="/wiki/California_Gold_Rush" title="California Gold Rush">California Gold Rush</a> of the mid-19th century;<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> in the 1890s it was estimated that in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Grass_Valley,_California" title="Grass Valley, California">Grass Valley</a>, over 60 per cent of the population was Cornish.<sup id="cite_ref-Jack2_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack2-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It has a tradition of <a href="/wiki/Carol_(music)" title="Carol (music)">carols</a> stemming from the Cornish who settled the area as gold miners in the 19th century. The carols have become "the identity of the town", some of the members of the Grass Valley Cornish Carol Choir are descendants of the original Cornish settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-Jack3_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack3-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most migratory Cornish to the United States were classified as English or British, meaning that the precise number of <a href="/wiki/Cornish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish American">Cornish Americans</a> is difficult to estimate. The aggregate number of immigrants from Cornwall to the United States before World War I is suggested to be around 100,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Ther243_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ther243-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Cornwall" title="Culture of Cornwall">Culture of Cornwall</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stpiraninpenwith.JPG" class="image"><img alt="A street lined with shops is filled with hundreds of people. In the foreground are children wearing black vests each one defaced with a large white cross. The children surround a fiddler. In the background are spectators." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Stpiraninpenwith.JPG/220px-Stpiraninpenwith.JPG" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Stpiraninpenwith.JPG/330px-Stpiraninpenwith.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Stpiraninpenwith.JPG/440px-Stpiraninpenwith.JPG 2x" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stpiraninpenwith.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St._Piran%27s_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Piran&#39;s Day">St. Piran\'s Day</a> is an annual patronal <a href="/wiki/Cornish_festivals" title="Cornish festivals">Cornish festival</a> celebrating Cornish culture and history every 5 March.</div></div></div>\n<p>The survival of a distinct <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Cornwall" title="Culture of Cornwall">Cornish culture</a> has been attributed to Cornwall\'s geographic isolation.<sup id="cite_ref-Mina482_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina482-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mina480_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina480-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Contemporaneously, the underlying notion of Cornish culture is that it is distinct from the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_England" title="Culture of England">culture of England</a>, despite its <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation" title="Anglicisation">anglicisation</a>, and that it is instead part of a Celtic tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> According to American academic <a href="/wiki/Paul_Robert_Magocsi" title="Paul Robert Magocsi">Paul Robert Magocsi</a>, modern-day Cornish activists have claimed several <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> inventions including the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_engine" title="Cornish engine">Cornish engine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christmas_carol" title="Christmas carol">Christmas carols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_football" title="Rugby football">rugby football</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brass_band" title="Brass band">brass bands</a> as part of this Cornish tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Cornish cultural tradition is most strongly associated with the people\'s most historical occupation, mining,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> an aspect of Cornish history and culture that has influenced its cuisine, symbols and identity.\n</p><p>Cornwall has <a href="/wiki/List_of_Cornish_saints" title="List of Cornish saints">its own tradition of Christian saints</a>, derived from Celtic extraction, that have given rise to localised dedications.<sup id="cite_ref-sny157_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sny157-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Saint_Piran" title="Saint Piran">Saint Piran</a> is the 5th century Christian abbot, supposedly of Irish origin, who is patron saint of both tin miners and Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-Saint_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saint-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> According to popular mythology, Piran, an Irish scholar who studied Christianity in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a> was to be drowned in the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a> by the <a href="/wiki/High_King_of_Ireland" title="High King of Ireland">High Kings of Ireland</a>, but instead floated across to <a href="/wiki/Perranporth" title="Perranporth">Perranporth</a> in Cornwall by the will of God to preach the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">ministry of Jesus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Saint_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saint-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Saint_Piran%27s_Flag" title="Saint Piran&#39;s Flag">Saint Piran\'s Flag</a>, a centred white cross on a black field,<sup id="cite_ref-Flag_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flag-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> was described as the "Standard of Cornwall" in 1838 and was re-introduced by <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Revival" title="Celtic Revival">Celtic Revivalists</a> thereafter as a <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_flags" title="List of English flags">county flag</a> of Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-Flag_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flag-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> It has been seized upon by the Cornish people as a symbol of their identity, displayed on cars and flying from buildings including those of <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">Cornwall Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-revagain_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revagain-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Far_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Far-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/St_Piran%27s_Day" title="St Piran&#39;s Day">St Piran\'s Day</a> is an annual patronal <a href="/wiki/F%C3%AAte" title="F\xc3\xaate">f\xc3\xaate</a>, and the pre-eminent <a href="/wiki/Cornish_festivals" title="Cornish festivals">Cornish festival</a> celebrating Cornish culture and history on 5 March.<sup id="cite_ref-Saint_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saint-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish language</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Cornish" title="Anglo-Cornish">Anglo-Cornish</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Country_dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="West Country dialects">West Country dialects</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Cornish_dialect_words" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Cornish dialect words">List of Cornish dialect words</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg/220px-Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg/330px-Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="364" data-file-height="305" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Penzansagasdynerghcrop.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Welcome_sign" title="Welcome sign">welcome sign</a> to <a href="/wiki/Penzance" title="Penzance">Penzance</a>, in the English and <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a> languages</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish language</a> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">Insular Celtic languages</a>. It is closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton language</a>, and to a lesser extent shares commonalities with the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh language</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> although they are not <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually intelligible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The language functioned as a community language in Cornwall until a <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">language shift</a> to the English language was completed during the late 18th century. The demise of the Cornish language is attributed to English cultural influence, particularly the political and religious dominance of the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Uniformity_1549" title="Act of Uniformity 1549">Act of Uniformity 1549</a> which outlawed all church services within the Kingdom of England that were not in English.<sup id="cite_ref-Pitt122_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pitt122-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The exact date of the death of using the Cornish language is unclear and disputed, but popularly it is claimed that the last <a href="/wiki/Monolingualism" title="Monolingualism">monolingual</a> Cornish speaker was <a href="/wiki/Dolly_Pentreath" title="Dolly Pentreath">Dolly Pentreath</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Mousehole" title="Mousehole">Mousehole</a> resident who died in 1777.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The revival of Cornish began in 1904 when <a href="/wiki/Henry_Jenner" title="Henry Jenner">Henry Jenner</a>, a Celtic language enthusiast, published his book <i>Handbook of the Cornish Language</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball644_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball644-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> He based his work on Cornish as it was spoken in the 18th century, although his pupil <a href="/wiki/Robert_Morton_Nance" title="Robert Morton Nance">Robert Morton Nance</a>, with his orthography, <a href="/wiki/Unified_Cornish" title="Unified Cornish">Unified Cornish</a>, later steered the revival more towards the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Cornish" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Cornish">Middle Cornish</a> that had been used in the 16th century, before the language became more heavily influenced by English.<sup id="cite_ref-mina486_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mina486-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> This set the tone for the next few decades; as the revival gained pace, learners of the language disagreed on which style of Cornish to use, and a number of competing orthographies\xe2\x80\x94Unified Cornish, <a href="/wiki/Unified_Cornish_Revised" class="mw-redirect" title="Unified Cornish Revised">Unified Cornish Revised</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modern_Cornish" title="Modern Cornish">Modern Cornish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kernewek_Kemmyn" title="Kernewek Kemmyn">Kernewek Kemmyn</a>\xe2\x80\x94were in use by the end of the 20th century. A <a href="/wiki/Standard_Written_Form" title="Standard Written Form">standard written form</a> was agreed in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cornish is a restored and living <a href="/wiki/Modern_language" title="Modern language">modern language</a>, but most of its speakers are enthusiasts, persons who have learned the language through private study.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball645_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball645-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Cornish speakers are geographically dispersed, meaning there is no part of Cornwall where it is spoken as a community language.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball645_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball645-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> As of 2009, it is taught in fifty primary schools,<sup id="cite_ref-revagain_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revagain-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> although regular broadcast in Cornish is limited to a weekly bilingual programme on <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_Cornwall" title="BBC Radio Cornwall">BBC Radio Cornwall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ball645_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball645-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Daily life in Cornwall therefore is conducted in the English language, albeit with <a href="/wiki/List_of_Cornish_dialect_words" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Cornish dialect words">some regional peculiarities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ther243_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ther243-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Legends of the Fall</i>, a novella by American author <a href="/wiki/Jim_Harrison" title="Jim Harrison">Jim Harrison</a>, detailing the lives of a <a href="/wiki/Cornish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish American">Cornish American</a> family in the early 20th century, contains several Cornish language terms. These were also included in the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> winning <a href="/wiki/Legends_of_the_Fall" title="Legends of the Fall">film of the same name</a> starring <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Hopkins" title="Anthony Hopkins">Anthony Hopkins</a> as Col. William Ludlow and <a href="/wiki/Brad_Pitt" title="Brad Pitt">Brad Pitt</a> as Tristan Ludlow.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_folklore">Literature and folklore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Literature and folklore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_literature" title="Cornish literature">Cornish literature</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Britain" title="Matter of Britain">Matter of Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historia_Regum_Britanniae" title="Historia Regum Britanniae">Historia Regum Britanniae</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early medieval</a> Cornwall was associated with the <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Britain" title="Matter of Britain">Matter of Britain</a>, a <a href="/wiki/National_myth" title="National myth">national myth</a> recounting a legendary Celtic history of Brittonic warriors, including <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mina482_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina482-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rough360_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rough360-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The Matter of Britain was supported by texts such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Regum_Britanniae" title="Historia Regum Britanniae">Historia Regum Britanniae</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Pseudohistory" title="Pseudohistory">pseudohistorical</a> account of the history of the ancient Britons, written in the mid-12th century by <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Monmouth" title="Geoffrey of Monmouth">Geoffrey of Monmouth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Consci1_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consci1-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>  The <i>Historia Regum Britanniae</i> chronicled the lives of <a href="/wiki/List_of_legendary_kings_of_Britain" title="List of legendary kings of Britain">legendary kings of the Britons</a> in a narrative spanning a time of two thousand years, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Trojans</a> founding the ancient British nation and continuing until the Anglo-Saxon invasion of Britain in the 5th century forced the Celtic Britons to the west coast, namely <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-Consci1_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consci1-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Denys_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denys-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Although broadly thought of as a work of fiction, Geoffrey of Monmouth\'s work had a lasting effect upon the identity of the Cornish.<sup id="cite_ref-Thom355_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thom355-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> His "historical construct" characterised the ancient Britons as heroes, which later helped Celtic revivalists to redefine Cornishness as an identity closely related to ancient heroic Celtic folklore.<sup id="cite_ref-Thom355_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thom355-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another strand of Cornish folklore is derived from tales of seafaring <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">pirates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smuggling" title="Smuggling">smugglers</a> who thrived in and around Cornwall from the early modern period through to the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Rough360_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rough360-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Cornish pirates exploited both their knowledge of the Cornish coastline as well as its sheltered creeks and hidden anchorages.<sup id="cite_ref-Rough360_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rough360-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> For many <a href="/wiki/Fishing_village" title="Fishing village">fishing villages</a>, loot and contraband provided by pirates supported a strong and secretive <a href="/wiki/Underground_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Underground economy">underground economy</a> in Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-Rough360_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rough360-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Legendary_creature" title="Legendary creature">Legendary creatures</a> that appear in Cornish folklore include <a href="/wiki/Bucca_(mythological_creature)" title="Bucca (mythological creature)">buccas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Knocker_(folklore)" title="Knocker (folklore)">knockers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pixie" title="Pixie">piskies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ste253_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ste253-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Tales of these creatures are thought to have developed as <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> explanations for the frequent and deadly <a href="/wiki/Cave-in" title="Cave-in">cave-ins</a> that occurred during 18th-century Cornish tin mining, or else a creation of the oxygen-starved minds of exhausted miners who returned from the underground.<sup id="cite_ref-Ste253_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ste253-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performing_and_visual_arts">Performing and visual arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Performing and visual arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_dance" title="Cornish dance">Cornish dance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Cornwall" title="Music of Cornwall">Music of Cornwall</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_obby_oss_maiden_20050502.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Red_obby_oss_maiden_20050502.jpg/220px-Red_obby_oss_maiden_20050502.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Red_obby_oss_maiden_20050502.jpg/330px-Red_obby_oss_maiden_20050502.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Red_obby_oss_maiden_20050502.jpg/440px-Red_obby_oss_maiden_20050502.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_obby_oss_maiden_20050502.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/%27Obby_%27Oss_festival" title="&#39;Obby &#39;Oss festival">\'Obby \'Oss festival</a> is a Cornish May Day festival celebrated in <a href="/wiki/Padstow" title="Padstow">Padstow</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Celtic_cross" title="Celtic cross">Celtic crosses</a>, many dating from between the 7th and 15th centuries, are found in Cornwall and have been used as inspiration in modern and contemporary Cornish visual arts.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>  In the 1780s, <a href="/wiki/John_Opie" title="John Opie">John Opie</a> was the first Cornish-born painter to gain widespread attention; his work was exhibited at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a> and he was described by <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Reynolds" title="Joshua Reynolds">Joshua Reynolds</a> as "like <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Vel\xc3\xa1zquez">Vel\xc3\xa1zquez</a> in one".<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Artists who appreciated the quality of Cornwall\'s <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">natural light</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">J. M. W. Turner</a>, began to visit, with more following after the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Western_Railway" title="Great Western Railway">Great Western Railway</a>, including <a href="/wiki/James_Abbott_McNeill_Whistler" title="James Abbott McNeill Whistler">Whistler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Sickert" title="Walter Sickert">Sickert</a>. <a href="/wiki/Stanhope_Forbes" title="Stanhope Forbes">Stanhope Forbes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Bramley" title="Frank Bramley">Frank Bramley</a> settled in Cornwall in the 1880s, establishing the <a href="/wiki/Newlyn_School" title="Newlyn School">Newlyn School</a> of painting <i><a href="/wiki/En_plein_air" title="En plein air">en plein air</a></i>. By the 1920s, the <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_art" title="Ceramic art">ceramicist</a> <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Leach" title="Bernard Leach">Bernard Leach</a> was established at <a href="/wiki/St_Ives,_Cornwall" title="St Ives, Cornwall">St Ives</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/St_Ives_School" title="St Ives School">St Ives School</a> for abstract artists formed there, influenced by <a href="/wiki/Naive_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Naive art">naive</a> painters such as <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Wallis" title="Alfred Wallis">Alfred Wallis</a>, and involving the work of <a href="/wiki/Ben_Nicholson" title="Ben Nicholson">Ben Nicholson</a>, his wife <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Hepworth" title="Barbara Hepworth">Barbara Hepworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naum_Gabo" title="Naum Gabo">Naum Gabo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Heron" title="Patrick Heron">Patrick Heron</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Cornish_saints" title="List of Cornish saints">List of Cornish saints</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Cornwall" title="Christianity in Cornwall">Christianity in Cornwall</a></div>\n<p>Anciently, the religion of the Cornish Britons was <a href="/wiki/Celtic_polytheism" title="Celtic polytheism">Celtic polytheism</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a> faith, assumed to be led by <a href="/wiki/Druid" title="Druid">Druids</a> in full or in part.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a> is thought to have existed in Cornwall during the 1st century, but limited to individual travellers and visitors, possibly including <a href="/wiki/Priscillian" title="Priscillian">Priscillian</a>, a Galician theologian who may have been exiled to the <a href="/wiki/Isles_of_Scilly" title="Isles of Scilly">Isles of Scilly</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Orme6_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orme6-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christianity</a> was introduced to Cornwall in the year 520 by <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petroc" title="Saint Petroc">Saint Petroc</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Magnag2_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnag2-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> a Brython from the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Glywysing" title="Glywysing">Glywysing</a>, and other missionaries from Wales, as well as by <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> monks and holy women from Ireland;<sup id="cite_ref-Rough360_29-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rough360-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> this "formative period" has left a legacy of granite <a href="/wiki/High_cross" title="High cross">high cross</a> monuments throughout Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-Rough360_29-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rough360-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Dedications to many different <a href="/wiki/List_of_Cornish_saints" title="List of Cornish saints">Cornish saints</a> can also be traced to this period.<sup id="cite_ref-Rough360_29-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rough360-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In the Middle Ages, Roman Catholicism was dominant in Cornwall,<sup id="cite_ref-Mina482_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina482-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and even in the 17th century the Cornish were "fervently Roman Catholic", slow to accept the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, according to some scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-Minahan180_110-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minahan180-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The adoption of <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> was, eventually, near-universal in Cornwall and facilitated the <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation" title="Anglicisation">anglicisation</a> of the Cornish people.<sup id="cite_ref-Tann230_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tann230-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> A variety of dissenting congregations such as the Quakers and Baptists were to be found in certain districts. Through a combination of tours of Cornwall by <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a>, rural isolation and compatibility with Cornish tastes and sensibilities,<sup id="cite_ref-Hemp27_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hemp27-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wesleyanism" title="Wesleyanism">Methodism</a>, an evangelical revival movement within the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Methodist_Central_Hall_Westminster_-_Methodism_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Methodist_Central_Hall_Westminster_-_Methodism-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UMCofWB_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UMCofWB-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> became the form of Christianity practised by the majority of the population all over Cornwall during the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Mina482_17-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina482-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hemp27_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hemp27-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> During this time other kinds of Methodist churches appeared such as the Bible Christians and there were also Evangelical and <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Movement" title="Oxford Movement">Anglo-Catholic</a> revivals within the Church of England.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_cuisine" title="Cornish cuisine">Cornish cuisine</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornish_pasty.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="A light brown pastry case is on a white plate" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Cornish_pasty.jpeg/220px-Cornish_pasty.jpeg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Cornish_pasty.jpeg/330px-Cornish_pasty.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Cornish_pasty.jpeg/440px-Cornish_pasty.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="1848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornish_pasty.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A  <a href="/wiki/Pasty" title="Pasty">Cornish pasty</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Cornish cuisine is a regional variety of <a href="/wiki/British_cuisine" title="British cuisine">British cuisine</a>, strongly rooted in a tradition of using local produce,<sup id="cite_ref-Tre6_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tre6-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> which is used to create relatively simple dishes.<sup id="cite_ref-Tre8_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tre8-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Most prominent in Cornish cuisine is the <a href="/wiki/Pasty" title="Pasty">pasty</a> (sometimes known as the Cornish pasty) made from  diced beef, potato, onion and swede (commonly called \'turnip\' by the Cornish), enclosed within a pastry crust and then baked.<sup id="cite_ref-Treg16_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Treg16-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> One idea of its origins suggests that it evolved as a portable lunch for Cornish miners, the crust serving as a disposable handle that could be held by a miner\'s hand without soiling the filling.<sup id="cite_ref-Tre8_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tre8-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Fish was an important element of the Cornish diet, but international <a href="/wiki/Commercial_fishing" title="Commercial fishing">commercial fishing</a> was also well established by the 16th century, and tons of <a href="/wiki/Sardine" title="Sardine">pilchards</a> were exported from Cornwall to France, Italy and Spain every year.<sup id="cite_ref-Tre6_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tre6-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stargazy_pie" title="Stargazy pie">Stargazy pie</a> is an occasional festive Cornish dish with the heads of fish standing on their tails, originally <a href="/wiki/Pilchards" class="mw-redirect" title="Pilchards">pilchards</a>, piercing a pastry crust.<sup id="cite_ref-Tre8_154-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tre8-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Treg16_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Treg16-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Saffron_bun" title="Saffron bun">saffron bun</a>, also known as the tea treat bun, is a sweet bread with its origins in Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-Jupp231_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jupp231-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Cornwall" title="Sport in Cornwall">Sport in Cornwall</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DSCF3394_CROP.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Two men in combat appear on a green lawn in front of a shrubbery. Both men are wearing dark coloured shorts and cream coloured tops. One man has a tactical advantage, and is throwing his opponent head-first towards the ground." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/DSCF3394_CROP.jpg/170px-DSCF3394_CROP.jpg" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/DSCF3394_CROP.jpg/255px-DSCF3394_CROP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/DSCF3394_CROP.jpg/340px-DSCF3394_CROP.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1468" data-file-height="1815" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DSCF3394_CROP.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cornish_wrestling" title="Cornish wrestling">Cornish wrestling</a> is a contact sport, a style of folk martial arts, that has its origins in Cornwall</div></div></div>\n<p>With its comparatively small, rural population, major contribution by the Cornish to national <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Sport in the United Kingdom">sport in the United Kingdom</a> has been limited.<sup id="cite_ref-Clegg10_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clegg10-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> There are no teams affiliated to the <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_County_Football_Association" title="Cornwall County Football Association">Cornwall County Football Association</a> that play in the <a href="/wiki/Football_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Football League">Football League</a> of <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_County_Cricket_Club" title="Cornwall County Cricket Club">Cornwall County Cricket Club</a> plays as one of the <a href="/wiki/Minor_counties_of_English_cricket" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor counties of English cricket">minor counties of English cricket</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Clegg10_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clegg10-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Viewed as an "important identifier of ethnic affiliation", <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> has become a sport strongly tied with notions of Cornishness,<sup id="cite_ref-Harv221_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harv221-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> and since the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union_in_Cornwall" title="Rugby union in Cornwall">rugby union in Cornwall</a> has emerged as one of the most popular spectator and team sports in Cornwall, with professional Cornish rugby footballers being described as a "formidable force",<sup id="cite_ref-Clegg10_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clegg10-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> "naturally independent, both in thought and deed, yet paradoxically staunch English patriots whose top players have represented England with pride and passion".<sup id="cite_ref-CornishRug_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CornishRug-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> In 1985, sports journalist <a href="/wiki/Alan_Gibson" title="Alan Gibson">Alan Gibson</a> made a direct connection between love of rugby in Cornwall and the ancient parish games of hurling and wrestling that existed for centuries before rugby officially began.<sup id="cite_ref-CornishRug_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CornishRug-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cornish_wrestling" title="Cornish wrestling">Cornish wrestling</a> (also known as Wrasslin\')<sup id="cite_ref-Harv221_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harv221-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> is a regional, folk style of <a href="/wiki/Grappling" title="Grappling">grappling</a> or martial arts. The Cornish Wrestling Association was formed in 1923, to standardise the rules of the sport and to promote Cornish wrestling throughout Cornwall and the world.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Together with <a href="/wiki/Cornish_hurling" title="Cornish hurling">Cornish hurling</a> (a localised form of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_football" title="Medieval football">medieval football</a>), Wrasslin\' has been promoted as a distinctly Celtic game, tied closely with Cornish identity.<sup id="cite_ref-Harv221_157-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harv221-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Surfing" title="Surfing">Surfing</a> was popularised in Cornwall during the late 20th century, and has since become readily associated with Cornishness.<sup id="cite_ref-Harv221_157-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harv221-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-surfbbc_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-surfbbc-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> The waves around the Cornish coastline are created by low pressure systems from the Atlantic Ocean which unleash powerful swells eastwards creating multiple, excellent surfing conditions in some parts of the coast of Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-surfbbc_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-surfbbc-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Newquay" title="Newquay">Newquay</a>, one of Britain\'s "premier surfing towns", regularly hosts world championship surfing events.<sup id="cite_ref-Harv221_157-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harv221-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-surfbbc_160-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-surfbbc-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutions_and_politics">Institutions and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Institutions and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Cornwall" title="Politics of Cornwall">Politics of Cornwall</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lostwithiel_-_the_Guildhall_-_geograph.org.uk_-_571361.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Lostwithiel_-_the_Guildhall_-_geograph.org.uk_-_571361.jpg/220px-Lostwithiel_-_the_Guildhall_-_geograph.org.uk_-_571361.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Lostwithiel_-_the_Guildhall_-_geograph.org.uk_-_571361.jpg/330px-Lostwithiel_-_the_Guildhall_-_geograph.org.uk_-_571361.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Lostwithiel_-_the_Guildhall_-_geograph.org.uk_-_571361.jpg/440px-Lostwithiel_-_the_Guildhall_-_geograph.org.uk_-_571361.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="429" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lostwithiel_-_the_Guildhall_-_geograph.org.uk_-_571361.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The surviving part of the former <i>Duchy Palace</i> in <a href="/wiki/Lostwithiel" title="Lostwithiel">Lostwithiel</a>, the former administrative seat of the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Cornwall" title="Duke of Cornwall">Duke of Cornwall</a> from c.1265 to 1874.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_County_Hall_Truro.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Old_County_Hall_Truro.jpeg/220px-Old_County_Hall_Truro.jpeg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Old_County_Hall_Truro.jpeg/330px-Old_County_Hall_Truro.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Old_County_Hall_Truro.jpeg/440px-Old_County_Hall_Truro.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_County_Hall_Truro.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Old County Hall in <a href="/wiki/Truro" title="Truro">Truro</a>, the former seat of <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">Cornwall Council</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Cornwall" title="Politics of Cornwall">politics of Cornwall</a> take place within a wider national political framework of a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>, in which the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Monarchy of the United Kingdom">United Kingdom\'s monarch</a> is <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>, and the supranational framework of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. Cornish politics are marked by a long tradition of Liberalism.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi379_23-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi379-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tre99_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tre99-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Important historical institutions were the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Cornwall" title="Duchy of Cornwall">Duchy of Cornwall</a> and the Cornish <a href="/wiki/Stannary_Courts_and_Parliaments" class="mw-redirect" title="Stannary Courts and Parliaments">Stannary Courts and Parliaments</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brithist_77-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brithist-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The Stannary court administered <a href="/wiki/Equity_(law)" title="Equity (law)">equity</a>, through special laws and legal exemptions, for all matters relating to the tin mines and tin trade in Cornwall. Cornish miners were effectively exempt from the jurisdiction of the law courts at <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Westminster</a>, except "in such cases as should affect land, life or limb".<sup id="cite_ref-Brithist_77-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brithist-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The ancient privileges of the Stannary Courts and Parliaments were confirmed by successive <a href="/wiki/Royal_Charter" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Charter">Royal Charters</a> in the Middle Ages, including those administered by Kings <a href="/wiki/John_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="John of England">John</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brithist_77-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brithist-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> As the tin mines of Cornwall lost their economic importance during the 18th and 19th centuries, so the Stannary institutions lost political power. The last Stannary parliament was held at <a href="/wiki/Truro" title="Truro">Truro</a> in 1752, and continued, by adjournments, until 11 September 1753.<sup id="cite_ref-Brithist_77-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brithist-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As in the rest of Great Britain, the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> dominated Cornish politics during the 19th century,<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> although Socialism gained limited support in western Cornwall,<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> won preference after World War I.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Nationalism (or <a href="/wiki/Regionalism_(politics)" title="Regionalism (politics)">regionalism</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-Schr106_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schr106-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> in Cornwall traces its roots to the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_bills" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule bills">Irish Home Rule bills</a> of the late 19th century,<sup id="cite_ref-Tre99_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tre99-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> and is represented by the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_self-government_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish self-government movement">Cornish self-government movement</a>, a political action group that is predominantly organised to promote Cornwall as the national homeland of the Cornish, campaign for <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a>, and win it the status as a fifth <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">country within the UK</a> rather than outright <a href="/wiki/Separatism" title="Separatism">separatism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mina483_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina483-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> More "militant" variants of Cornish nationalism however claim that because of historical constitutional peculiarities regarding the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_status_of_Cornwall" title="Constitutional status of Cornwall">status of Cornwall</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Law of the European Union">law of the European Union</a> does or should not have jurisdiction over Cornwall until Cornish sovereignty is recognised.<sup id="cite_ref-Mina483_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina483-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Popularisation of Cornish nationalism is attributed to a Celtic cultural revival in Cornwall which itself began with a newed interested in the Cornish language in the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-Mina483_166-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina483-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The revival of the Cornish language encouraged a parallel revival of Celtic traditions, which by the 1970s had spurred on Cornish nationalism.<sup id="cite_ref-Mina483_166-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina483-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The United Kingdom\'s entry into the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> in 1973 prompted claims that the Cornish should be granted their own <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved</a> <a href="/wiki/National_assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="National assembly">national assembly</a>\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Assembly" title="Cornish Assembly">Cornish Assembly</a>\xe2\x80\x94comparable to that of the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">National Assembly for Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schr106_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schr106-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mina483_166-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mina483-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mebyon_Kernow" title="Mebyon Kernow">Mebyon Kernow</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">left-wing political party</a> based in Cornwall, founded in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-Mebyon_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mebyon-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Its main objective is attaining greater autonomy for Cornwall through the establishment of a legislative Cornish Assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> As at 2009 Mebyon Kernow has no Members of Parliament elected to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons of the United Kingdom</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_2009" title="United Kingdom local elections, 2009">United Kingdom local elections, 2009</a> received 4 per cent of votes to elect councillors to <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">Cornwall Council</a>, behind the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> (34 per cent), <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> (28 per cent), and <a href="/wiki/Independent_(politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Independent (politician)">Independents</a> (23 per cent)<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Since the <a href="/wiki/2009_structural_changes_to_local_government_in_England" title="2009 structural changes to local government in England">2009 structural changes to local government in England</a>, Cornwall Council has been a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authorities_of_England" title="Unitary authorities of England">unitary authority</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> serving as the sole executive, <a href="/wiki/Deliberation" title="Deliberation">deliberative</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Legislative" class="mw-redirect" title="Legislative">legislative</a> body responsible for local policy, setting <a href="/wiki/Council_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Council tax">council tax</a>, and allocating budgets.\n</p><p>Following the Cornwall Council election in May 2013, the council remained as "no overall control", with the Independent politicians becoming the largest grouping on the council through a modest gain of councillors from the previous election. The Liberal Democrats remained the second largest party after losing 2 councillors and the Conservatives slipped to third after losing over a third of their councillors. The Labour Party (+8), UKIP (+6), and the Green Party (+1) all gained seats, with UKIP and the Greens entering Cornwall Council for the first time. Mebyon Kernow had 6 councilors prior to the election, having added 2 since the 2009 election, their total following the election was reduced to 4.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 general election</a> all Cornish seats were won by the Conservatives. This was repeated in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="United Kingdom general election, 2017">2017 general election.</a>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section:
